The rolls began at 50 seconds into the engine burn. The burn was stopped 11 seconds early after burning a total of 76 seconds. After engine cutoff, the craft continued rolling while coasting to apogee. The roll was finally brought under control after apogee using the craft\'s reaction jets. SpaceShipOne landed safely and Mike Melvill was uninjured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Separation failure\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap">19 April 2008</span>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TMA-11" title="Soyuz TMA-11">Soyuz TMA-11</a>\n</td>\n<td>Reentry mishap similar to that suffered by Soyuz 5 in 1969. The service module failed to completely separate from the reentry vehicle and caused it to face the wrong way during the early portion of aerobraking. As with Soyuz 5, the service module eventually separated and the reentry vehicle completed a rough but survivable landing. Following the Russian news agency <a href="/wiki/Interfax" title="Interfax">Interfax</a>\'s report, this was widely reported as life-threatening<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> urged caution pending an investigation of the vehicle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> South Korean astronaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yi_So-Yeon" title="Yi So-Yeon">Yi So-Yeon</a> was hospitalized after her return to South Korea due to injuries caused by the rough return voyage in the Soyuz TMA-11 spacecraft. The South Korean Science Ministry said that the astronaut had a minor injury to her neck muscles and had bruised her spinal column.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Aborted spacewalk after water leak in suit\n</td>\n<td><span class="nowrap">16 July 2013</span>\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a> <a href="/wiki/Expedition_36" title="Expedition 36">Expedition 36</a>\n</td>\n<td>During EVA-23, <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> astronaut <a href="/wiki/Luca_Parmitano" title="Luca Parmitano">Luca Parmitano</a> reported that water was steadily leaking into his helmet. Flight controllers elected to abort the EVA immediately, and Parmitano made his way back to the <a href="/wiki/Quest_Joint_Airlock" title="Quest Joint Airlock"><i>Quest</i> airlock</a>, followed by fellow astronaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chris_Cassidy" title="Chris Cassidy">Chris Cassidy</a>. The airlock began repressurizing after a 1-hour and 32 minute spacewalk, and by this time Parmitano was having difficulty seeing, hearing, and speaking due to the amount of water in his suit. After repressurization, Expedition 36 commander <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Vinogradov" title="Pavel Vinogradov">Pavel Vinogradov</a> and crewmembers <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Yurchikhin" title="Fyodor Yurchikhin">Fyodor Yurchikhin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Karen_Nyberg" title="Karen Nyberg">Karen Nyberg</a> quickly removed Parmitano\'s helmet and soaked up the water with towels. Despite the incident, Parmitano was reported to be in good spirits and suffered no injury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> By December, 2013, NASA had determined the leak to have been caused by a design flaw in the Portable Life Support System liquid coolant. The designers failed to take into account the physics of water in zero-g, which unintentionally allowed coolant water to mix with the air supply.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Hole detected in station\n</td>\n<td>30 August 2018\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soyuz_MS-09" title="Soyuz MS-09">Soyuz MS-09</a> attached to <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">ISS</a> <a href="/wiki/Expedition_55" title="Expedition 55">Expedition 55</a>\n</td>\n<td>Ground controllers detected a dip in cabin pressure, which astronauts traced to a 2-millimeter hole in <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_MS-09" title="Soyuz MS-09">Soyuz MS-09</a>, which was quickly patched up by Soyuz commander Sergey Prokopyev with epoxy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Launch booster failure, ballistic re-entry\n</td>\n<td>11 October 2018\n</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soyuz_MS-10" title="Soyuz MS-10">Soyuz MS-10</a>\n</td>\n<td>The crew reported feeling weightless; mission control declared a rocket had failed. An emergency was declared and the spacecraft carrying the crew was separated from the rocket. It returned to Earth in a ballistic descent (sharper than normal angle), and the crew experienced 6.7 <a href="/wiki/G-force" title="G-force">G</a> during the landing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn1_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn1-80">[80]</a></sup> The crew did not need immediate medical care when recovered. Investigation determined the ball joint supporting one of the side boosters had been deformed during assembly; the damaged joint prevented proper separation despite proper activation of the separation motors; the booster re-contacted the core stage, inflicting further damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-fatal_incidents_during_training">Non-fatal incidents during training</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Non-fatal incidents during training">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LLTV_crash.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="350" data-file-width="378" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/LLTV_crash.jpg/220px-LLTV_crash.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/LLTV_crash.jpg/330px-LLTV_crash.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/7b/LLTV_crash.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LLTV_crash.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Test pilot Stuart Present ejects safely from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LLRV" title="LLRV">Lunar Landing Training Vehicle</a>. Neil Armstrong also made such an ejection. (NASA)</div></div></div>\n<p>Spaceflight-related accidents and incidents during assembly, testing, and preparation for flight of manned and unmanned spacecraft have occasionally resulted in injuries or the loss of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Craft_(vehicle)" title="Craft (vehicle)">craft</a> since the earliest days of space programs.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_other_incidents">List of other incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: List of other incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="1960.E2.80.931969"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1960&#8211;1969">1960&#8211;1969</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1960&#8211;1969">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>Three of the five <a href="/wiki/Lunar_Landing_Research_Vehicle" title="Lunar Landing Research Vehicle">Lunar Landing Research and Training</a> vehicles (LLRV and LLTV) were destroyed in crashes near Houston, Texas:\n<ul><li>1968 May 6: LLRV No. 1 crashed at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ellington_AFB" title="Ellington AFB">Ellington AFB</a>, Texas, caused by loss of helium pressure that controlled the steering jets. Neil Armstrong ejected safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1968 December 8: LLTV No. 1 crashed at Ellington AFB, Texas, caused by failure of the fly-by-wire control system. MSC test pilot Joseph Algranti ejected safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1971 January 29: An LLTV crashed at Ellington AFB, Texas, caused by failure of the fly-by-wire control system. NASA test pilot Stuart Present ejected safely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></li></ul></li>\n<li>1960 July 16: <i>Injured during centrifuge training</i>&#160;: Soviet cosmonaut Anatoly Yakovlevich Kartashov suffered pinpoint hemorrhages of the spine during centrifuge training. Due to the injuries he was grounded by the medical staff and retired from the cosmonaut group on 7 April 1962<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1961 August 19: <i>Parachute training accident, broken left foot</i>&#160;: Cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Belyayev" title="Pavel Belyayev">Pavel Belyayev</a> broke his left foot during a parachute jump related to cosmonaut training. As a result, he was out of cosmonaut training until 30 August 1962.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1961 Sep 2: <i><a href="/wiki/Lockheed_T-33" title="Lockheed T-33">T-33</a> jet engine failure, emergency landing</i>&#160;: Astronauts Gus Grissom and Alan Shepard experienced a broken rotor blade in the jet engine of their T-33 jet while flying a training mission over Lake Erie. Grissom made an emergency landing at Selfridge Air Force Base near Detroit, MI. Both astronauts were unharmed and the aircraft was not further damaged. The astronauts left in the same jet a day later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1962 Oct 22: <i>Experimental Paraglider, hard landing</i>&#160;: Astronaut Gus Grissom was piloting an experimental paraglider at Edwards Air Force Base, CA, that was towed aloft by another aircraft and released. The paraglider made a hard landing that crumpled the nose wheel. The craft remained upright and Grissom walked away unhurt. The craft was part of experiments that were to lead up to landing Gemini spacecraft using a similar paraglider wing on dry land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1963 March 13: <i><a href="/wiki/Convair_F-102_Delta_Dagger" title="Convair F-102 Delta Dagger">F-102</a> jet, ran off paved runway</i>&#160;: Astronaut Elliot See was piloting a NASA F-102 fighter jet used to maintain astronaut pilot proficiency when it ran off the end of the paved runway while landing at Ellington AFB near Houston, TX. Officials said the astronaut landed the plane too fast and was unable to stop on the paved portion of the runway. Astronaut See was not injured and the aircraft was not damaged and was flown later in the day by another astronaut.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1964 Aug 25&#160;: <i>Simulated Moon Walk in Spacesuit, fall on steep, rocky lava bed</i>&#160;: Astronaut Walter Cunningham was wearing a full spacesuit with 13.5&#160;kg backpack. He was simulating a moon walk on a rocky lava bed near Bend, Oregon. While climbing a 15-meter, 30 degree slope, he was 3 meters from the top when he fell backward. At first, observers thought he might roll all the way to the bottom of the slope, but Cunningham spread out to slow his motion and a nearby engineer also provided assistance. Cunningham was uninjured, but there was a slight pressure loss in the suit from a glove puncture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1964 Nov 4&#160;: <i><a href="/wiki/Northrop_T-38_Talon" title="Northrop T-38 Talon">T-38</a> jet, ran off wet runway, landing gear damaged</i>&#160;: Astronauts Charles "Pete" Conrad and James Lovell were landing at Ellington AFB near Houston, TX, during a rainstorm, on a flight from Washington, DC. They were returning from the funeral of astronaut Theodore C. Freeman. They were unable to stop their aircraft on the wet runway. The aircraft ran off the runway and into a muddy, grassy area damaging the landing gear. The two astronauts were not injured in the mishap.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1966 Jan 14&#160;: <i>Altitude Chamber, oxygen valve explosion, fire</i>&#160;: Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Edward_Givens" title="Edward Givens">Edward Givens</a> was testing the Gemini 9 astronaut maneuvering unit (AMU) while wearing a spacesuit in an altitude chamber at the Manned Spacecraft Center (Johnson Space Center), Houston, TX. While in the altitude chamber, an oxygen valve exploded outside the altitude chamber burning four technicians and sending one of them to the hospital. One of the technicians\' clothes was set on fire and the other three technicians suffered minor burns extinguishing the burning clothing. Astronaut Givens was not injured in the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1966 January 28&#160;: <i>Zero G Training, dislocated shoulder requiring surgery</i>&#160;: Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Donn_F._Eisele" title="Donn F. Eisele">Donn F. Eisele</a> underwent surgical repair of his left shoulder due to a dislocation received during zero G flight training in 1965. The shoulder was reinjured during physical training at the Manned Spacecraft (Johnson Space) Center later the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1966 July 18&#160;: <i>T-38 jet takeoff abort, ran off runway</i>&#160;: Astronauts Edward H. White and Russell Schweickart experienced an engine failure during takeoff from El Paso International airport, TX, on their T-38 jet aircraft. The takeoff was aborted and the aircraft ran off the end of the runway, suffering a nose gear collapse and blowouts of both main landing gear tires. The astronauts were flying from Houston, TX to Los Angeles, CA. The stop in El Paso was to refuel. Both astronauts were uninjured and continued their journey on a commercial airliner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1966 October 8: <i>Parachute training accident, broken foot</i>&#160;: Cosmonaut Georgi Grechko broke his foot during a parachute jump related to training for the Soyuz 7K manned lunar flyby missions. The injury forced him out of training for those lunar flyby missions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1968 May 15&#160;: <i>Parachute training, fracture</i>&#160;: Astronaut Dr. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Robert_A._R._Parker" title="Robert A. R. Parker">Robert A. R. Parker</a> suffered a fractured coccyx (the final bone in the spine), while taking part in parachute training. The injury occurred at Williams Air Force Base, Arizona. The injury was not thought to be serious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1968 September 26&#160;: <i>Physical training, fractured collar bone</i>&#160;: Astronaut Dr. Karl G. Henize suffered a fractured collar bone during a physical development class as part of astronaut training. He was placed on non-flying status for about five weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1969&#160;: <i>Rope training accident, serious leg injury</i>&#160;: Cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Kovalyonok" title="Vladimir Kovalyonok">Vladimir Kovalyonok</a> suffered a serious injury to his leg while rope training (climbing?) during cosmonaut training. He recovered and was able to continue cosmonaut training and graduated 18 August 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1969 Aug 2&#160;: <i>helicopter landing accident</i>&#160;: During NASA astronaut helicopter flight training, astronaut Edward G. Gibson, flying solo, landed a helicopter on a mud flat, near Laporte, TX. The helicopter sank in the mud, flipped over and its spinning rotor blades tore the craft apart. Gibson was uninjured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1969 Aug 15: <i>T-33 jet crash landing</i>&#160;: Astronaut Joseph Kerwin made a <a href="/wiki/Belly_landing" title="Belly landing">belly landing</a> on a foam covered runway in his T-33 jet trainer at Ellington AFB near Houston, Texas due to a landing gear problem. He survived the crash landing uninjured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id="1970.E2.80.931979"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1970&#8211;1979">1970&#8211;1979</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1970&#8211;1979">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>1971 January 23: <i>helicopter crash</i>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eugene_Cernan" title="Eugene Cernan">Eugene Cernan</a> was flying a <a href="/wiki/Bell_47" title="Bell 47">Bell 47G</a> helicopter as part of his Lunar Module training as Backup Commander for <a href="/wiki/Apollo_14" title="Apollo 14">Apollo 14</a>. The helicopter crashed into the <a href="/wiki/Indian_River_(Florida)" title="Indian River (Florida)">Indian River</a> at <a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Florida" title="Florida">Florida</a>. Cernan nearly drowned because he was not wearing a life vest and received some second-degree burns on his face and singed hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> According to official reports at the time, the crash was the result of mechanical failure. Later accounts, written by Cernan himself in an autobiography, admit he was flying too low and showing off for nearby boaters. The helicopter dipped a skid into the water and crashed. <a href="/wiki/James_McDivitt" title="James McDivitt">James McDivitt</a>, an Apollo Manager at the time, demanded that Cernan be removed from flight status and not be given command of <a href="/wiki/Apollo_17" title="Apollo 17">Apollo 17</a>. Cernan was defended by Deke Slayton and given the Apollo 17 command. James McDivitt resigned as an Apollo Manager shortly after the Apollo 16 mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1971 Apr 2&#160;: <i>T-38 jet, rear cockpit canopy lost, takeoff aborted</i>&#160;: Astronaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_Truly" title="Richard Truly">Richard Truly</a> was taking off from Kellogg Field, Battle Creek, MI in a NASA T-38 jet. During the takeoff roll, the rear cockpit canopy flew off the aircraft. Astronaut Truly aborted the takeoff and was not injured. The aircraft suffered minor damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1972 May 10&#160;: <i>T-38 jet, electrical malfunction, out of fuel, ejected</i>&#160;: Astronaut Charles "Pete" Conrad ejected safely from his NASA T-38 jet near Bergstrom AFB, Austin, TX. He landed about 90 meters from the base operations building. An electrical malfunction that caused loss of instruments during severe weather was listed by the review board as a major factor in the accident. Conrad was on a flight from Dover, DE via Dobbins AFB, GA to Ellington AFB, Houston, TX. Due to bad weather, he was diverted first to Hobby Airport, Houston because Ellington AFB was below minimums for landing. While on final approach to Hobby Airport, in darkness, heavy rain and lightning, the T-38 generator failed, causing a loss of cockpit lighting and partial loss of navigation instruments. Conrad broke off the approach and tried to climb above the bad weather. The generator was brought back online along with cockpit lighting. Because of the electrical problems, he requested a diversion to an airport that was under visual flight rules. Controllers sent him toward Randolph AFB, San Antonio, TX. It soon became apparent the T-38 did not have enough fuel to reach Randolph AFB. Controllers then diverted him to Bergstrom AFB. The T-38 ran out of fuel as it reached Bergstron AFB. Conrad ejected at 1100 meters. The aircraft was destroyed. He was taken to the base hospital for a routine examination and returned to Houston later that night.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1974 Feb 6&#160;: <i>T-38 jet, landing mishap, gear collapse</i>&#160;: Astronaut Dr. Karl G. Henize was involved in a "landing mishap" in his T-38 jet aircraft while landing at Bergstrom AFB near Austin, TX in low visibility conditions. He was on an instrument flight from Ellington AFB near Houston, TX. A landing gear on the T-38 collapsed and the aircraft was damaged. Henize was not injured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1979 October 19: <i>Altitude chamber accident, electrical burns, brain concussion</i>&#160;: Cosmonaut Alexander S. Viktorenko was conducting tests in an altitude chamber as part of cosmonaut training. Due to errors by a person operating the altitude chamber, Viktorenko was struck by an electric current, causing burns, a fall and a brain concussion. He was unconscious for 17 hours. As a result of the injuries and recovery, he did not complete his cosmonaut training until 24 February 1982.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id="1980.E2.80.931989"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1980&#8211;1989">1980&#8211;1989</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1980&#8211;1989">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>1982 May 21: <i>T-38 jet struck by lightning, damaged</i>&#160;: Astronaut Gordon Fullerton was piloting a solo flight of a NASA T-38 jet aircraft from Houston, Texas to Cleveland, Ohio to make a speaking engagement. While on final approach to Cleveland\'s Hopkins Airport, his aircraft was struck by lightning. Fullerton landed safely but a post-landing inspection showed that a 2-foot by 6-foot section had been torn from the tail of the aircraft. The astronaut later commented, "It felt like a howitzer hit the cockpit. It was as strong a lightning (bolt) as I\'ve ever experienced.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1982 Dec 1: <i>T-38 jet landing accident, ran off runway</i>&#160;: Astronaut Thomas K. Mattingly was not injured when his T-38 jet aircraft ran off the runway at Ellington AFB near Houston, Texas. The aircraft ended up 30 feet off the end of the runway during a landing in heavy rain. There was substantial damage to the wings, nose and landing gear of the aircraft during the incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1984 Apr 5&#160;: <i>T-38 jet, struck birds, engine flameout, aborted takeoff</i>&#160;: Astronaut <a href="/wiki/James_van_Hoften" title="James van Hoften">James van Hoften</a> was taking off in a T-38 jet on the KSC Space Shuttle runway for a training flight. This was training for the <a href="/wiki/STS-41-C" title="STS-41-C">STS-41-C</a> mission of Challenger that launched the next day. At about 1.5&#160;km (1&#160;mi) down the 4.8&#160;km (3&#160;mi) runway, while going 260&#160;km/h (162&#160;mph), his jet struck a flock of birds, causing the right engine to flame out. He applied the brakes and safely aborted takeoff without further aircraft damage. Bird remains were later found on the nose landing gear and the aircraft engine needed to be removed and inspected for further damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1987 Feb 24: <i>T-38 jet engine failure, fire, emergency landing</i>&#160;: Astronaut Brewster Shaw and NASA pilot Robert Rivers experienced an engine failure and fire in their T-38 jet aircraft. The jet was on approach to Los Alamitos Army Air Field, CA, when the right engine failed and caught fire. There was smoke in the cockpit. The crew chose to land the plane rather than bail out because it was over a populated area. They landed successfully and climbed out of the burning jet.  The jet experienced substantial damage. The crew was taken to the Long Beach Naval Hospital for observation and later released.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1989 May 15&#160;: <i>T-38 jet, near midair collision with airliner</i>&#160;: Astronaut <a href="/wiki/David_M._Walker_(astronaut)" title="David M. Walker (astronaut)">David M. Walker</a> was flying a NASA T-38 jet into Dulles Airport near Washington, D.C. for a presidential ceremony. At about 8&#160;km (5&#160;mi) from the airport at an altitude of 2100 meters, he came within 150 meters of colliding with a Pan Am <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airbus_310" title="Airbus 310">Airbus 310</a> airliner. He was later found at fault for the incident and suspended from flying for 60 days by NASA. He was also removed as commander of the <a href="/wiki/STS-44" title="STS-44">STS-44</a> mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id="1990.E2.80.931999"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1990&#8211;1999">1990&#8211;1999</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1990&#8211;1999">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>1993 May 3&#160;: <i>Emergency egress training, broken bones in foot</i>&#160;: Astronaut Dr. M. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhea_Seddon" title="Rhea Seddon">Rhea Seddon</a> broke four metatarsal bones in her left foot during emergency egress training from the Johnson Space Center orbiter training facility.  She was using an inflatable slide similar to those used on airliners during landing emergency evacuations. While sliding down the slide, her left foot became pinned under her, breaking four minor bones. She returned to full-time training after a few weeks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1993 May 28&#160;: <i>frostbitten fingers in thermal vacuum chamber</i>&#160;: While training in a thermal vacuum chamber for the <a href="/wiki/STS-61" title="STS-61">STS-61</a> Hubble repair mission, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Story_Musgrave" title="Story Musgrave">Story Musgrave</a> suffered frostbite on the fingertips of his right hand. He was working in a spacesuit in the chamber for about six hours at low temperatures. His fingertips were blackened and numb from the incident. Surgery was not required and they were sufficiently healed in time for the repair mission in December 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></li>\n<li>1993 Oct 16: <i>Medical experiment, heart and breathing stopped</i>&#160;: Astronaut <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bonnie_Dunbar" title="Bonnie Dunbar">Bonnie Dunbar</a> suffered an allergic reaction to an experimental drug and collapsed during medical tests at Johnson Space Center, Texas. Her breathing and heart stopped and she was rushed to a local hospital. She recovered and was later declared in good health. The experiment involved injecting dye and a drug to measure the effect of weightlessness on fluids in the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id="2000.E2.80.932009"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2000&#8211;2009">2000&#8211;2009</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 2000&#8211;2009">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li>2000 Mar 15&#160;: <i>sprained ankle delays shuttle launch</i>&#160;: Astronaut and commander of the <a href="/wiki/STS-101" title="STS-101">STS-101</a>  mission, <a href="/wiki/James_D._Halsell" title="James D. Halsell">James D. Halsell</a> sprained his ankle climbing down steps inside of a space shuttle simulator at Johnson Space Center, Houston. This caused him to miss some training activities and delayed the launch of his mission by about a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2003 Dec 2&#160;: <i>NASA Gulfstream II Shuttle Training Aircraft - engine thrust reverser fell off aircraft in flight</i>&#160;: A NASA Gulfstream II shuttle Training Aircraft (STA) was flying a series of simulated shuttle landings to the Kennedy Space Center shuttle landing facility. On board the aircraft was an unidentified NASA astronaut pilot and two training personnel. The aircraft was on final approach at 13,000 feet when on-board instruments indicated a malfunction on one of the jet engine thrust reversers. The aircraft landed safely. A post-landing inspection showed that one of the 585-pound, 4-foot-wide, 5-foot-long thrust reversers had fallen off the aircraft. Divers later found the thrust reverser on the bottom of the nearby Banana River. An investigation showed that a bolt failed, causing the part to fall off the aircraft.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup></li>\n<li>2003 Dec 17&#160;: <i>SpaceShipOne - Landing accident, ran off runway</i>&#160;: While piloting <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne" title="SpaceShipOne">SpaceShipOne</a> on its first powered test flight, <a href="/wiki/SpaceShipOne_flight_11P" title="SpaceShipOne flight 11P">11P</a>, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Brian_Binnie" title="Brian Binnie">Brian Binnie</a> reached a peak altitude of 20.7&#160;km (13&#160;mi) and exceeded the speed of sound. Upon landing, SpaceShipOne experienced a roll oscillation that caused the left main gear to collapse. The craft ran off the runway and rolled to a stop in soft sand. The craft sustained minor damage, later repaired, and the pilot was uninjured.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span id="2010.E2.80.932019"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="2010&#8211;2019">2010&#8211;2019</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: 2010&#8211;2019">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable plainlinks" role="note">This list is <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:WikiProject_Lists#Incomplete_lists" title="Wikipedia:WikiProject Lists">incomplete</a>; you can help by <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit">expanding it</a>.</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-astronaut_fatalities">Non-astronaut fatalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Non-astronaut fatalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fatalities_caused_by_rocket_explosions">Fatalities caused by rocket explosions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Fatalities caused by rocket explosions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>This list excludes deaths caused by military operations, either by deliberate detonations, or accidental during production - for example German V-2 rockets reportedly caused on average an estimated 6 deaths per operational rocket just during its production stages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Date</th>\n<th>Place</th>\n<th>Dead</th>\n<th>Rocket</th>\n<th>Description\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1930-05-17</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Weimar_Republic" title="Weimar Republic">Germany</a></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Max_Valier" title="Max Valier">Max Valier</a>, "first casualty of the modern space age",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> killed by rocket engine explosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1931-02-02</td>\n<td>Mount Redoria near <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>A liquid fueled, 132-pound (60&#160;kg) <a href="/wiki/Sounding_rocket" title="Sounding rocket">meteorological rocket</a>, that was constructed by American physicist, Dr. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Darwin_Lyon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Darwin Lyon (page does not exist)">Darwin Lyon</a>, exploded during tests, killing a mechanic and injuring three others. Dr. Lyon was not present when the explosion occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span href="/wiki/Reinhold_Tiling" title="Reinhold Tiling">Reinhold Tiling</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1934-07-16</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kummersdorf" title="Kummersdorf">Kummersdorf</a>, Germany</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Aggregat_(rocket_family)#A2_(1934)" title="Aggregat (rocket family)">A2</a></td>\n<td>Research project under the supervision of <a href="/wiki/Walter_Dornberger" title="Walter Dornberger">Walter Dornberger</a> killed Kurt Wahmke and two assistants as part of the <a href="/wiki/Aggregat_(rocket_family)" title="Aggregat (rocket family)">Aggregat_</a> rocket development,  during a fuel test of a premixed hydrogen peroxide/alcohol propellant when the fuel tank exploded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1960-10-24</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Kazakh Soviet Socialist Republic">Kazakh SSR</a></td>\n<td>78</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/R-16_(missile)" title="R-16 (missile)">R-16</a></td>\n<td>The <a href="/wiki/Nedelin_catastrophe" title="Nedelin catastrophe">Nedelin catastrophe</a> caused by ignition of second-stage engines on the pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1963-10-24</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Kazakh Soviet Socialist Republic">Kazakh SSR</a></td>\n<td>7-8</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/R-9_Desna" title="R-9 Desna">R-9 Desna</a></td>\n<td>On the same day as the Nedelin catastrophe, another catastrophe took place: due to the evaporation of fuel and a short circuit, a fire took the lives of 7<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> or 8<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> people. Since then, 24 October is considered a "Black Day", and Russia has not launched rockets on that day.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1964-04-14</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>, US</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delta_rocket" title="Delta rocket">Delta rocket</a></td>\n<td>The third stage of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delta_rocket" title="Delta rocket">Delta rocket</a> had just been joined to the <a href="/wiki/Orbiting_Solar_Observatory" title="Orbiting Solar Observatory">Orbiting Solar Observatory</a> satellite in the spin test facility building at Cape Kennedy. Eleven workers were in the room when the 205&#160;kg of solid fuel in the third stage ignited. Sidney Dagle, 29; Lot D. Gabel, 51, and John Fassett, 30, were severely burned and later died of their injuries. Eight others were injured, but survived. The ignition was caused by a spark of <a href="/wiki/Static_electricity" title="Static electricity">static electricity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1964-05-07</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Braunlage" title="Braunlage">Braunlage</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a></td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Rocket_mail" title="Rocket mail">Mail rocket</a></td>\n<td>Mail rocket built by <a href="/wiki/Gerhard_Zucker" title="Gerhard Zucker">Gerhard Zucker</a> exploded and debris hit crowd of spectators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1966-12-14</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a>, USSR</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soyuz_7K-OK" title="Soyuz 7K-OK">Soyuz 7K-OK</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soyuz_7K-OK_No.1" title="Soyuz 7K-OK No.1">Soyuz 7K-OK No.1</a>: Second unmanned <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> test flight. Launch escape system fired 27 minutes after an aborted launch causing a fire and subsequent explosion when pad workers had already returned to the launch pad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESiddiq2000874_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESiddiq2000874-144">[144]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1973-06-26</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Plesetsk_Cosmodrome" title="Plesetsk Cosmodrome">Plesetsk Cosmodrome</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">USSR</a></td>\n<td>9</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kosmos-3M" title="Kosmos-3M">Kosmos-3M</a> launch vehicle</td>\n<td>Launch explosion of <a href="/wiki/Kosmos-3M" title="Kosmos-3M">Kosmos-3M</a> rocket\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1980-03-18</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Plesetsk_Cosmodrome" title="Plesetsk Cosmodrome">Plesetsk Cosmodrome</a>, USSR</td>\n<td>48</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Vostok-2M" title="Vostok-2M">Vostok-2M</a> launch vehicle</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/1980_Plesetsk_launch_pad_disaster" title="1980 Plesetsk launch pad disaster">Explosion while fueling up</a> a <a href="/wiki/Vostok-2M" title="Vostok-2M">Vostok-2M</a> rocket<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1990-09-07</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edwards_AFB" title="Edwards AFB">Edwards AFB</a>, CA <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Titan_IV" title="Titan IV">Titan IV</a></td>\n<td>A <a href="/wiki/Titan_IV" title="Titan IV">Titan IV</a> launch vehicle solid rocket booster was being hoisted by a crane into a rocket test stand at Edwards AFB, California. The bottom section of the booster broke free, hit the ground and ignited. One person, Alan M. Quimby, 27, a civilian employee of Wyle Laboratories, was killed and 9 others were injured in the accident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1991-08-09</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komaki,_Aichi" title="Komaki, Aichi">Komaki, Aichi</a>, Japan</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/H-II" title="H-II">H-II</a> launch vehicle</td>\n<td>Engineer Arihiro Kanaya, 23, was conducting a high pressure endurance test on a pipe used in the first stage rocket engine of the H-2 (H-II) launch vehicle when it exploded. The explosion caused a 14&#160;cm thick door in the testing room to fall on Kanaya and fracture his skull, killing him. The accident happened at the <a class="extiw" href="https://ja.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E4%B8%89%E8%8F%B1%E9%87%8D%E5%B7%A5%E6%A5%AD%E5%90%8D%E5%8F%A4%E5%B1%8B%E8%AA%98%E5%B0%8E%E6%8E%A8%E9%80%B2%E3%82%B7%E3%82%B9%E3%83%86%E3%83%A0%E8%A3%BD%E4%BD%9C%E6%89%80" title="ja:&#19977;&#33777;&#37325;&#24037;&#26989;&#21517;&#21476;&#23627;&#35480;&#23566;&#25512;&#36914;&#12471;&#12473;&#12486;&#12512;&#35069;&#20316;&#25152;">Nagoya Guidance and Propulsion Systems Works Of</a> <a href="/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries" title="Mitsubishi Heavy Industries">Mitsubishi Heavy Industries</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Komaki,_Aichi" title="Komaki, Aichi">Komaki, Aichi</a>, Japan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1993-02-27</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Esrange" title="Esrange">Esrange</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Sweden</a></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Nike-Orion" title="Nike-Orion">Nike-Orion</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup></td>\n<td>Bror Thorn&#233;us, a technician from Sweden was killed when a sounding rocket ignited during testing of its ignition system at the European Sounding Rocket Range (<a href="/wiki/Esrange" title="Esrange">Esrange</a>), in northern Sweden.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1995-01-26</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Xichang" title="Xichang">Xichang</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a></td>\n<td>6+</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rocket" title="Long March rocket">Long March rocket</a></td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rocket" title="Long March rocket">Long March rocket</a> veered off course after launch <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span class="nowrap">1996-02-15</span></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Xichang" title="Xichang">Xichang</a>, China</td>\n<td>6-100</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long_March_rocket" title="Long March rocket">Long March rocket</a></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Intelsat_708" title="Intelsat 708">Intelsat 708 Satellite</a>, a Long March rocket, veered off course immediately after launch, crashing in the nearby village 22 seconds later. and destroying 80 houses. According to official Chinese reports there were 6 fatalities and 57 injuries resulting from the incident, but other accounts estimated 100 fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2002-10-15</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Plesetsk_Cosmodrome" title="Plesetsk Cosmodrome">Plesetsk Cosmodrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Soyuz-U" title="Soyuz-U">Soyuz-U</a></td>\n<td>A <a href="/wiki/Soyuz-U" title="Soyuz-U">Soyuz-U</a> exploded 29 seconds after launch, killing a soldier, Ivan Marchenko, and injuring 8 others. during a nitrous oxide injector test<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_non-astronaut_fatalities">Other non-astronaut fatalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_spaceflight-related_accidents_and_incidents&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other non-astronaut fatalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Date</th>\n<th>Place</th>\n<th>Dead</th>\n<th>Associated mission/vehicle</th>\n<th>Description\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1968-05-16</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>, US</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Apollo_4" title="Apollo 4">Apollo 4</a></td>\n<td>Pad worker William B. Estes, 46, was killed while hooking up an 8-inch (20&#160;cm) high-pressure water line to the mobile service structure on Kennedy Space Center <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_39" title="Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39</a>A, which should not have been pressurized at the time. The cap blew off with 180 psi pressure, striking him in the chest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worker_Plunges_To_Death_At_Cape_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worker_Plunges_To_Death_At_Cape-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1981-03-19</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>, US</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a></td>\n<td><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anoxia" title="wiktionary:anoxia">Anoxia</a> due to nitrogen atmosphere in the aft engine compartment of <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia">Columbia</a></i> during a <a href="/wiki/Terminal_countdown_demonstration_test" title="Terminal countdown demonstration test">countdown demonstration test</a> for <a href="/wiki/STS-1" title="STS-1">STS-1</a>. Five workers were involved in the incident. John Bjornstad died at the scene; Forrest Cole went into a coma and died two weeks later, and Nick Mullon died 14 years later from complications of injuries sustained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1981-05-05</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Kennedy_Space_Center" title="Kennedy Space Center">Kennedy Space Center</a>, US</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/STS-2" title="STS-2">STS-2</a></td>\n<td>Construction worker Anthony E. Hill, 22, fell more than 100 feet (30&#160;m) from the Kennedy Space Center <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_39" title="Launch Complex 39">Launch Complex 39</a>B service structure. Workers were preparing LC-39B for a planned September 1981 launch of the Space Shuttle <i>Columbia</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worker_Plunges_To_Death_At_Cape_158-1"><a href="#cite_note-Worker_Plunges_To_Death_At_Cape-158">[158]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1985-12-04</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vandenberg_AFB" title="Vandenberg AFB">Vandenberg AFB</a>, US</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>Carl Reich, 49, of Lompoc, CA, an iron worker employed by Hensel Phelps Construction of Greeley, CO, fell 18 stories from the mobile service structure of the SLC-6 Space Shuttle launch complex, while bolting a platform onto the structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1988-05-04</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Henderson,_NV" title="Henderson, NV">Henderson</a>, Nevada, US</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/PEPCON_disaster" title="PEPCON disaster">PEPCON disaster</a>, explosion of a factory that produced <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_perchlorate" title="Ammonium perchlorate">ammonium perchlorate</a> for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">solid-fuel rocket</a> boosters of the Space Shuttle and other launchers.\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1989-12-22</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>, US</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>A worker refurbishing the 11th level of the Cape Canaveral, Atlas Launch Complex 36B launch tower, was killed when an air hose he was using was caught by the pad elevator. The hose wrapped around the worker and pulled him into the elevator shaft, crushing him. The pad was being refurbished for commercial satellite launches by General Dynamics starting in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>1995-05-05</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Guiana_Space_Centre" title="Guiana Space Centre">Guiana Space Centre</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_Guiana" title="French Guiana">French Guiana</a></td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ariane_5" title="Ariane 5">Ariane 5</a></td>\n<td>Two technicians died from <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/anoxia" title="wiktionary:anoxia">anoxia</a> due to major nitrogen leak in confined area of umbilical mast at Ariane 5 launch area during cryogenic M1 main stage testing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2001-07-08</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>, US</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>Worker disconnecting a coupling on a temporary pipe used to purge a liquid oxygen system near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_37" title="Launch Complex 37">Launch Complex 37</a>. Unexpected buildup of pressure caused the coupling to break loose and strike the employee in the head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen1003_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-sen1003-169">[169]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><span class="nowrap">2001-10-01</span></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Cape_Canaveral" title="Cape Canaveral">Cape Canaveral</a>, US</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>Crane operator Bill Brooks was killed in an industrial accident at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Complex_37" title="Launch Complex 37">Launch Complex 37</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sen1003_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-sen1003-169">[169]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2002-05-12</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a></td>\n<td>8</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Buran_(spacecraft)" title="Buran (spacecraft)">Space Shuttle Buran</a></td>\n<td>Workers repairing the roof of the <a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a> N-1/<a href="/wiki/Energia" title="Energia">Energia</a> vehicle assembly building died when the roof suffered a total structural collapse and crashed 80 meters (260&#160;ft) to the ground. Buran Shuttle was destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2004-02-24</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Satish_Dhawan_Space_Centre" title="Satish Dhawan Space Centre">Satish Dhawan Space Centre</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></td>\n<td>6</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>After curing process of an experimental solid propellant segment weighing 14.5 tonnes, during removal of bottom plate from casting assembly, propellant within segment caught fire resulting in death of four engineers and two assistants. Three workers escaped the inferno with burn injuries. Cast Cure facility building suffered extensive damage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2010-05-05</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Redstone_Arsenal" title="Redstone Arsenal">Redstone Arsenal</a>, US</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td></td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Ammonium_perchlorate" title="Ammonium perchlorate">Ammonium perchlorate</a> explosion in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solid-fuel_rocket" title="Solid-fuel rocket">solid rocket fuel</a> test area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2011-03-14</td>\n<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Launch_Pad_39A" title="Launch Pad 39A">Launch Pad 39A</a>, USA</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a></td>\n<td>A person working as a swing-arm contractor fell to his death during preparations for a Space Shuttle mission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2013-11-09</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Plesetsk" title="Plesetsk">Plesetsk</a>, Russia</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>Two workers cleaning out a propellant tank died when exposed to poisonous nitrogen tetroxide gases within the tank<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sputniknews_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-sputniknews-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SpaceFlightNow_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-SpaceFlightNow-179">[179]</a></sup>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>2017-06-14</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a></td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Progress_MS-06" title="Progress MS-06">Progress MS-06</a></td>\n<td>An ISS resupply mission, debris from the launch caused a wildfire which killed one worker employed to recover rocket debris. Another employee was injured and died a few days afterward in hospital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span
